# Offensive Penetration Testing

> As a pentester, you need to understand the methods of real-life attackers and use the tools, techniques, and resources they exploit. Join ethical hacker Clint Kehr as he guides you from basic hacking concepts to advanced exploitation techniques in this Offensive Penetration Testing Course. Develop the hacker mindset or hone your existing skills. This course is part of a Professional Certificate fr

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing) | 9h 36m | Intermediate | 94K learners

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/offensive-penetration-testing-course-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** Welcome to the Cybrary OnDemand Offensive [Penetration Testing](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Penetration%20Testing.md) Course. My name is Clint Kehr, and I'll be your tour guide. Why should you take this course? Well, this course is a very hands-on course. I don't want it to be a death by [PowerPoint](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20PowerPoint.md) course. I want to demonstrate the techniques that ethical hackers or Pentesters are currently using, as well as you getting your hands dirty in labs. We have a bunch of labs in this course, and you'll get to put hands on keyboard and demonstrate these hacker concepts yourself. Also to harness the hacker mindset. What do I mean by that? Well, if you're like me and you like Legos or you have kids that like Legos, let's say you get your Legos and you're missing a couple pieces, what do you do? How do you think around that problem? That's kind of the hacker mindset in a nutshell. It's the ability to look at a problem and figure out ways around the problem or working with the existing conditions that you have, which may not be ideal, but figuring out ways to move laterally or thinking laterally. Also, I want to talk about Pentesting certifications, not the boring multiple choice ones, but the ones where you actually have to demonstrate that you're a hacker, that you're a good hacker. And I think these certifications get your foot in the door when it comes time to get a Pentesting role. And I can kind of attribute that to the role I'm in now. I think the [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) was a great way to get my foot in the door in my current role. So like I said, this is going to be a very hands-on course. You'll get a chance to watch me demonstrate the techniques and then you do it yourself.

> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/offensive-penetration-testing-course-overview?u=76281980&t=95)** And then I think that that is what is going to get your foot in the door. Like I said, maybe you're already a Pentester, maybe you're a beginner Pentester or intermediate Pentester who kind of wants to, you know, I talk some about the basics of Pentesting, and then I kind of leap more into the advanced techniques or intermediate techniques, I should say, in Pentesting. So if you're a beginner, you may have some problems following along with some of the concepts, just because I kind of move quickly through the concepts. But if you need to brush up or your intermediate, I think this course would be perfect for you. [Cybersecurity](../../Topics/Cybersecurity.md) professionals, that's a very ambiguous term. But if you're already in the industry and you want to learn more about hacking techniques, this is a good course for you. And network administrators. I think the best hackers have that [Network Administration](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Network%20Administration.md) background because as hackers, right, we need to basically emulate or model our techniques off of what network admins do. Right. We want to see what users are on the system. We want to look at networking within the system. We have to maybe modify a firewall rule. So kind of what we do as hackers model, what network admins do. So we're talking about these certifications, these hacking certifications.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/offensive-penetration-testing-course-overview?u=76281980&t=188)** There's a lot of different organizations out there, [CEH](../../Glossary/Concept/CEH.md) being EC-Councils. And I want to also point out the fact that, yes, it is a multiple-choice test, but they've kind of moved also into a hands-on version of the CEH as well, which I think is great. I think any hands-on test is great. So I applaud EC-Council for also offering a hands-on version of the CEH. eLearnSecurity is newer, but I've taken the eWPT and the [eCPPT](../../Glossary/Concept/eCPPT.md) exams and both of those are seven days in an environment to hack and then seven days to write a report. I think that's incredibly realistic and also very beneficial. As an employer, if I have someone that I know is able to hack into a virtual environment and write a good penetration test report, maybe that's someone I want to hire because I know that they have done that before and are certified in doing that. So if you've taken a SANS course, GIAC is the organization that you take your test through. GIAC is moving also towards a more hands-on version of certification. So if you've taken GPEN recently, it's not only multiple choice questions, but also some VMs that you have to load and answer the questions from the VMs. So CompTIA is known for security+. I think if you're new to cybersecurity, Security+ is the certification for you. CompTIA also has PenTest + which I think is their version of the CEH. Now, there are multiple-choice tests or questions in this test,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/offensive-penetration-testing-course-overview?u=76281980&t=283)** but they also have demonstration questions where maybe you have to rank things or, you know, which steps 1, 2, and 3, what steps come first, second, and third. So that's demonstrating that you have some understanding of the techniques. And of course, there's the OSCP. Right. Which is, of course, out of all of these, is the biggest certification, foundational certification, offensive security calls it. But I think the one that demonstrates, one, not only that you have the skills to be a hacker, but also that you have the hacker mindset as well. So again, my name is Clint Kehr. I was a police officer and federal agent for about 14 years and also a Navy reservist before becoming an ethical hacker. And I think you'll see a lot of law enforcement officers kind of gravitate more towards the forensics side. Right. Not too many law enforcement officers are hackers or become hackers. So I really enjoyed when I got into cybersecurity and my job as an agent. I really enjoyed the Pentesting part of it and decided one day, I want to do this full-time. I loved being an agent. I loved serving my country also as a Navy reservist, but I really, really loved Pentesting and it was kind of this leap of faith into the industry to get into a role where I can do Pentesting. So I'm very fortunate in the fact that I got to be a federal agent, I got to be a law enforcement officer. And I also get to, in my current role, protect people by doing [Ethical Hacking](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Ethical%20Hacking.md).

And also, equally important is writing a report. Right. Like I talked about with eLearn and also OSCP, writing the report is a vital component of the certification itself. And also, like I said, understanding the hacker mindset. The people that are very successful in this industry are those that don't have to follow steps one through five. They can think laterally, they can think around problems. And that ability, I think, is very important when it comes
>
> **[8:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/offensive-penetration-testing-course-overview?u=76281980&t=481)** to being a Pentester. So what should you already have? You should already have a solid understanding of TCP/IP networking. You should have a reasonable level of understanding of [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) and Linux. Maybe you've been in network admin, maybe you haven't. Maybe you just know the command line very well. I think that's a good baseline here. You should be familiar with Windows and the Linux command line like I just said, and also some understanding of Bash scripting and [Python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md) scripting as well. It's not imperative, but we're talking a lot about exploit scripts here, and it's not being a script kiddie and just using [Metasploit](../../Glossary/Framework/Metasploit.md) and firing things off from there, but also the fact that you can read scripts and understand what they do. So we're going to go through, like I said, from the very beginning of the foundations of success in these tests, these hands-on tests to setting up Kali Linux and then understanding network protocols. Like I said, we're going to look at Web app Pentesting, we're going to look at buffer overflows. We're going to look at public exploits. And what do I mean by that? I mean looking at the code of public exploits, modifying that to fit our environment and then launching attacks from there. Also, when you get a shell, what do you do next? And then privilege escalation, going from an unprivileged user to a privileged user as well as understanding how to crack passwords and conduct brute force login attacks. Then we're going to kind of put everything together with a [Capstone](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Capstone.md) lab that you'll have to do. So thank you for enrolling in this course. A journey of a thousand miles begins with a single step. This is your single step into this journey to get you ready for these hands-on Pentesting certifications that will either get your foot in the door to become a professional Pentester or help you become a better professional Pentester if you already are one.

### 1. Setting the Foundation for Success

#### [Understanding the penetration test report](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=0)** Welcome to Module 2: Setting the Foundation for Success. Lesson 1: Understanding the Penetration Test Report. Our learning objectives are to understand the components of the penetration test report as well as hacking the penetration test report for relevant information. You'll see books, guides, all kinds of things describing what makes a good Pentest report. At the very core, we have three different sections. One is an executive summary, so a high-level summary for executives describing what you found. So the totality of what you found, how severe it is, as well as can I fix this? If I'm an executive, I want to know, you know, what did you find, how bad is it, and how do I fix it? And in a very non-technical way. So as best as you can describe what you found without using any kind of technical jargon, just a general description goes a very long way as well as including things like graphs and charts that really helps display exactly what you found and how severe it might be. Methodologies are important, information gathering. At the end of [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), you don't want to realize that you didn't scan for every port and you didn't discover every service. Our scanning and enumeration portion of our Pentest is very, very, very critical. And it's important to include what you found in your penetration test report. You know, maybe Port 22 is open and they didn't want Port 22 to be open.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=94)** So that's important to tell your customer. Attack path as far as what vulnerability you exploited. This is very important information. Of course, you're basically going through your attack path of how you exploited the vulnerability and got a shell on a vulnerable machine. So if you can describe what that vulnerability is, if there's a CVE attached to that, that's great. You may find exploit code in something like [Exploit-DB](../../Glossary/Service/Exploit-DB.md). [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) is your best friend here as well as maybe you found something, a vulnerability in a custom piece of software. In offensive security, in their sample report, they have both both a CVE and something found in a custom piece of application. So if there's a specific CVE, of course, you can use that information from your Google searches. And then for custom applications, you may want to use something like a CWE, which is more of a broad category. So a CVE might be a [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) injection in a [WordPress](../../Skills/Web%20Development/WordPress.md) plugin, whereas a CWE is just generally what a SQL injection is. So you want to explain what the vulnerability is as well as how to fix it. If there's a CVE, you can be confident that the vendor has issued a patch or guidance on how to fix that. CWEs are great too, because they, you know, it's a SQL injection, but they'll provide some on the on the Mitre website for CWEs. They'll provide some general guidance on how to fix SQL injections. I know this meme is used a lot, but I don't think you have
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=190)** too many screenshots. That's not to say you should include all of them in your OSCP report. The worst thing, again, is to have your OSCP lab end, and you can no longer take screenshots and then realize that you didn't take enough. So when I include screenshots in my report, I will typically have a caption below and explain why I'm including that screenshot to show why it's relevant and why I'm including it in my Pentest report. There was an appendix back in the 2013 version of the PWK report on the OffSec website. I'm glad they took that out because it didn't really follow a logical flow. Now it really does. You have your executive summary and then it kind of goes through each host and explains what vulnerability was found, how severe it is, and then your path of exploitation. And it lays that out for each different host rather than having different sections broken out for. These are all my recommendations. These are all my attack paths. I don't think that was very clear. So I'm glad that the new version kind of breaks things out by specific host. A couple of bonuses here. If you already exploited those ten different hosts in the PWK lab, you've already had the experience of writing this. And you can take those, the good and the bad that you found writing that report into the OSCP report when you write it. So you'll already have some background of what worked for you and what didn't. If you finish up the OSCP early, if you're one of the lucky few to do that, make sure you go through your
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=285)** attack steps and clearly document that in your report. So my third OSCP attempt, I had plenty of time. So while I was writing my report, I was going through my attack steps within the lab environment, and that was very helpful. Also, if you go to the Offensive Security forums, they already have a write-up for two of their machines, Alpha and Beta. I would look through those and see how they're written because, of course, the people scoring your exam are the people that wrote those up. So see how they themselves wrote up their Pentest report, so to speak. Don't reinvent the wheel. You already have an exam or a sample report available to you on the Offensive Security website. I've seen other people come out with their own custom reports and things of that nature. I've used the Offensive Security report not only for the OSCP but for some other eLearn tests, and I found it to be very useful, and I was successful in those exams. So I really do like the Offensive Security report on their website. Also, if there's already a CVE out there, Google is your best friend, as I've said before. You know, research that CVE, try to find, you know, fixes for it, clear explanations of what it is. And if it's a custom piece of application, again, you can go back to CWEs, CAPEC is kind of like a more broad categories like CWEs but they have great descriptions of vulnerabilities, you know, cross-site scripting, whatever it might be, and general fixes on how to fix those types
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=379)** of vulnerabilities. Severity is also a big one. If I could go back and change the severity in my report, I would, because now that I'm in this realm, I didn't think or I don't think I scored my severity correctly. And I think that's very important, especially for customers. Customers are going to want to know if there's a critical vulnerability, they need to fix it immediately rather than maybe it's a low and then they have more time to fix that and they can focus on more of this severe vulnerabilities. So a lot of CVEs already have a severity assigned to them. But if not, if it's more of a general or custom piece of software, you can use CVSS to score that, and we'll show that in the demo. If you didn't get the local or proof file, you know, I've seen people go back and forth on this. Should I include that information in my report? I don't think it hurts to include this information. If you don't include it, you're definitely not going to get extra points. If you do include it, maybe they will give you extra points. But if I was a customer, I'd want to see that I'm paying for a quality Pentest. And even if, you know, even if the Pentester wasn't able to exploit something, maybe there's something that's concerning that I should know about, I still want that included in the report. So don't forget these things. The local and proof files must show the IP address. So you'll want to do ipconfig or IPA or ifconfig depending on what type of machine you have, you want to make sure you have both of those in your screenshots. Don't forget that because you'll you'll lose points. You want to submit your proof files in the control panel,
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-penetration-test-report?u=76281980&t=474)** you want to make sure you do that accurately. You want to make sure that you're accurately submitting the correct hash for the correct machine and the correct file. Follow the submission instructions very carefully. If you mix up hashes or you don't do something correctly, you could lose all your points for that machine even though you got root on it. So once you submit your lab report, you can't go back and submit it again. I tried this. I wrote the report, realized I should have included more and then resubmitted it. And they said, "No, we're only taking your first report." So you can get all the proofs. You can hack every single machine, but you could fail because you wrote a bad report. So the report is equally as important as compromising as many machines as you can. So, you know, pay attention to writing your lab report for the exam. So here's the quiz question. What screenshots must your report include? Original code and then modified code, local and proof files with hash and IP address or obtaining a shell in the box? So the correct answer is local and proof files with hash and IP address. In summary, we learned how to understand the components of the penetration test report, as well as how to hack the penetration test report itself for relevant information on what we need to include when we write our report for the OSCP.

And I wouldn't worry about these other scores down here, temporal or environmental. It's just stick to the base score comes out to be high. ### 2. Kali Linux Basics

Setting up the Kali Linux VM. Our learning objectives are to understand the types of virtual machine software available to run Kali Linux. So virtualization is kind of like inception. It's a computer within a computer. You have your host operating system, whether you have a Mac or you have [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) and then you have a computer within that using this virtualization software, which is really just a file, but it's a whole operating system contained within your computer. For example, people who analyze malware will do so in a virtual machine because the malware will infect the virtual machine, but not the host operating system. Or hopefully, it won't. The software that's available is Oracle's VirtualBox. There's also VMware Workstation player for Windows. There's also Pro versions. So there's VMware Fusion for Mac and VMware Workstation Pro for Windows. Because I do a lot of virtualization, I do pay for the Pro version of Fusion and Workstation. So if you want to do privilege escalation, the tmp directory is where you're going to bring your file into to execute that privilege escalation exploit. But something like a globally readable and writable /etc/passwd file is bad, really bad. History is also important because if you get on to a box as a user and you type in history, maybe you can see that user's history, sensitive file names, passwords, things like that. Change directory; that's important too. CD . is the same directory you're in. CD .. goes back a directory that becomes important with local file inclusion vulnerabilities. That's why you'll see ../, ../, ../ because we're trying to get back into maybe the /etc/passwd or /etc/directory. So we're going ../, ../, ../ to get all the way back into etc. Also, Linux is case sensitive. So cybrary, lowercase C and Cybrary, uppercase C are not the same file. They are different files, which is different from being a Windows user where it's case insensitive. Just know Linux is case sensitive. To make a file, you'll want to do the touch command. Touch; lowercase t. Touch New file creates that new file. Of course, we have the underscore there because if we had new space file, that would not be the same. It would not be one file. Make directory, M-K-D-I-R, same in Windows. So example make directory cybrary will make the cyber directory. Cat reads files; that's an important one to know. Cat command line way to read files. You can also create files so you'll see cat greater than cybrary.txt. You enter some text, hit "Enter." Ctrl D will create that file with that text.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-command-line?u=76281980&t=477)** Cyber is awesome in it. Remove files; rm Maybe we didn't like that new file. RM, space, the new file name will delete that file. Rmdir. Maybe we want to remove the Cybrary directory. If something's in it, though, it's not going to work. You have to do mrdir -r to recursively remove all the files in that directory. CP copies files. You can copy multiple files into one directory using CP. Also move; maybe we don't like the name new file you could do, move, the name "new file," and then call it old file and it should change the name to old file. So in summary, that was a very quick lesson, but now we understand why the command line is important for Pentesters. And also, we know a little bit more about the Linux file system.

### 3. Understanding Network Protocols

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Scanning network protocols](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-network-protocols?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-network-protocols?u=76281980&t=0)** Module 4: Understanding Network Protocols. Scanning Network Protocols. In this lesson, we're going to describe the tools available to scan network host for open ports and understand why we use network scanners as hackers. So hopefully, none of this is new to you. Network protocols is something -- is not a beginner course. So things like ICMP, TCP, UDP, these should all be familiar terms to you. But basically, it's great to interact with our own host. We can fire up the command prompt or the terminal and interact with our own host. But to pass [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), you need to be able to interact with a remote host. So you're going to have to use a network. So you'll, you know, maybe you'll call the helpdesk and you'll say, I want to reach [Google.com](https://Google.com). I'm having problems. And they'll say, you know, ping, [google.com](https://google.com), and let's see if those packets come back as lost or you're able to actually ping that host. That's using ICMP. Let's say you're able to figure out your networking issues and now you can ping [Google.com](https://Google.com) with no problem. Now, you want to open up a browser, so you're using your browser to go to [Google.com](https://Google.com). Well, [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) wants to be available to you and you want a reliable connection, so you'll use TCP, you'll use that three-way handshake. If you hear three-way handshake, it's referring to TCP. I'll send a SYN/ACK, send packet out to Google. It will send a SYN/ACK packet back and then I'll send an ACK packet and we'll just keep going back and forth with TCP to make sure that connection is reliable until I terminate that connection. UDP is a fire and forget protocol. So if I'm streaming video, if I'm streaming music,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-network-protocols?u=76281980&t=95)** I'll use UDP. It's a fast protocol. So let's say I'm looking at something like DNS. DNS uses both TCP and UDP. And if I'm using a network scanner, I want to scan DNS with both TCP and UDP. And that's to say that when I use a scanning tool, I'm looking for different services on different ports and ports are like doorways. So if I know that DNS is on Port 53, I'll scan for that. Maybe I'm looking for SSH on Port 22, but if you're like me and have your own home network and your own Raspberry Pi, you may change a common port like SSH to a different port. So there are commonly known ports or commonly used ports. You should be familiar with them. You should know what Port 80 is. You should know what Port 445 is, but also know that people may obfuscate or change ports on you. Also, I would recommend a course like Network+ or Security+ to kind of go over more of these protocols, what networking is. I think when I left law enforcement and got more into [Cybersecurity](../../Topics/Cybersecurity.md), what I was more comfortable with was networking. Just because as a hacker, I mean, you need to know about networking and how to interact with hosts across a network and know what all the different protocols are. So there are more scanning tools than just these three. But I just wanted to point out these three, [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md) being my favorite. Nmap has been around for a very long time. It's very reliable and has the Nmap scripting engine, which is very robust in finding various vulnerabilities and
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-network-protocols?u=76281980&t=193)** enumerating various services. I love Nmap. I think in OSCP, Nmap is perfect, but that's also to say that there is something like [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md). So Netcat isn't just used for things like getting a bind shell or getting a reverse shell. Netcat can also be used to scan different ports to see if they're open. There was a Hack The Box challenge where Nmap wasn't working for me, but Netcat was, and I found an open port using Netcat where it says it was closed with Nmap so I didn't want to discount Netcat. There's also Masscan. So Masscan, I have to say I've had a bit of problems with Masscan in the past. It is very fast, though. so when I was a special agent, I actually had a case where a company used Masscan and found something interesting. I can, of course, talk about that. But that's to say that, you know, Masscan is very fast. It takes -- it can scan the entire Internet in only six minutes. So if you want to use a fast tool, Masscan is definitely a go-to if you can get it to work. But the purpose of all this is to enumerate these hosts on the network. So here's our quiz question. Which network scanner claims it can scan the entire Internet in six minutes? Well, I just said it, so hopefully this is an easy one for you. Is it Nmap, Netcat, or Masscan? And the answer is Masscan. So in summary, we described the tools available to scan network host for open ports and understand why we use network scanners
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-network-protocols?u=76281980&t=289)** as hackers.

#### [Scanning with MASSCAN](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-with-masscan?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-with-masscan?u=76281980&t=0)** Scanning with Masscan. In this lesson, we're going to understand how to use Masscan to scan for open ports and services, as well as describe some of the pluses and minuses of the scanner. As I've mentioned before, Masscan is a very fast scanner. If your job is to scan a subnet very quickly or maybe the entire Internet very quickly at a certain port, this is the scanner for you. It doesn't work exactly like [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md). It doesn't do all that error checking. It doesn't look for all that feedback like Nmap does. That's why it can work so fast. So that's why I like Nmap better, I should say, in the context of [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), just because you're trying to get those granular details. If you're trying to scan for all open ports in the OSCP environment, you know, maybe use Masscan, but when you actually want to pull more information like banners and maybe some vulnerability checking, Nmap is the tool for you. So if we compare Masscan with Nmap, when you run Nmap, if you just do an Nmap in an IP address, it already looks for default ports. Masscan doesn't work that way. Masscan, you actually have to specify the ports. And that's why I was saying it's important to know the common ports because you know which ports to look for as opposed to searching from port 1-10,000. That's going to take a long time. If you're strategic and you search for ports like 21, 22, 445,139, if you know these common ports, then you can accomplish your task very quickly. Also, you can't use names like [Google.com](https://Google.com).
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/scanning-with-masscan?u=76281980&t=95)** It doesn't use DNS. You have to use actual IP addresses and IP ranges. And as you can see here, Nmap is a little bit more flexible in using subnet ranges, whereas Masscan is not. So when you run Masscan, it uses kind of these default configurations. And if we translate that to Nmap, it uses the Syn scanner, half-open scan. So if you run Nmap as a superuser, it uses this half-open scan by default. So it will send the syn packet out, it'll get the SYN/ACK packet back and it will never send that SYN/ACK packet back to that host. So it doesn't complete that three-way handshake. That means you have to use Masscan as a superuser. It also uses this no ping scan, so it treats every host as up. There's no DNS resolution that randomize the host, so it doesn't do it sequentially. And also it's using the raw libpcap. So it's at the data link layer rather than at the network layer. Sometimes, Nmap has to decide which one it wants data link layer or network layer. Masscan just uses the data link layer. So if you actually want to do banner checking, so the way Masscan works is it has its own TCP IP- and that's why it can run so quickly. So if you actually want to get banners back to your -- within your scan, you need to firewall a certain port or you need to specify an IP address in your subnet that is not actually a live host. #### [Using Wireshark](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-wireshark?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-wireshark?u=76281980&t=0)** Using [Wireshark](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Wireshark.md). In this lesson, we're going to understand the basic use of Wireshark as both an attacker and [defender](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Microsoft%20Defender.md), as well as to understand the traffic we generate with [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md). So Wireshark is an open-source packet capture tool and it also analyzes packets as well. It's used by system admins or network admins to debug networking issues. It's also used by the blue team guys, the defenders to look for malicious traffic and we can also use it as attackers to figure out what kind of noise our tools are making. Or maybe one of our tools isn't working and we want to know why. So we use Wireshark to kind of debug our own issues as well as understand perhaps we set up a server and we want to see what the hosts are that are coming to our website. and we'll see a little bit later how we can weaponize that, which is pretty fun. So Wireshark with Nmap. Again, I said it's important to know what your tools do over the wire. And we're going to look at two different scans with Nmap. There's the sT flag or the full three-way handshake, TCP handshake, and these are also the Syn scan or stealth scan. So I want to show you what that looks like over the wire in Wireshark. First, you want to pick your correct interface. So I use IP space A or if your old school ifconfig. I know that's deprecated now though, but I'm going to use my home network which is eth0. You may have a different network, especially if you're connected to a VPN, it may be tap or something
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-wireshark?u=76281980&t=93)** like that. So ensure that you pick the correct interface and you can either go to the top left and look for Wireshark in the search bar there or open up a terminal and type Wireshark and it should launch the application. So we're going to scan using that sT flag or the full three way handshake on Port 23. As you can see here, I'm the root user, so I'm doing Nmap and I'm specifying that sT flag. If you're the Kali user or the non-root user, it should do this by default, that three-way handshake. So you'll see here in Wireshark, I started my capture, which is the shark fin on the upper left-hand corner. And I'm also -- now I'm trying to drill down on that specific traffic on Port 23. You'll notice when you're capturing traffic in Wireshark, it's very noisy very quickly. So knowing how to filter is very important. So what I did was tcp.port == 23. And you can see here that means the attacker sends a SYN packet. We get a SYN/ACK packet back from that host, we send an ACK packet to do that full three-way handshake and then we send a reset ACK packet to terminate the connection. So now again, as the root user, if you're not the root user, you have to use sudo. But I'm not specifying any flags, so it should do the stealth scan or the Syn scan by default. Again, we're scanning Port 23.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-wireshark?u=76281980&t=187)** So here, you'll see that our host sends a SYN packet, we get a SYN/ACK packet back and then we send a reset packet to terminate that without finishing the three-way handshake. Again, I'm drilling down using the filter up there. And you can see here it says tcp.port == 23. So I'm just looking at the traffic going to Port 23, the TCP traffic going to Port 23, I should say. So per Nmap, they prefer the stealth scan or the syn scan. The irony being that a lot of defensive tools look for that nowadays. So they're actually looking for that stealth scan. So it's actually not very stealthy. You know, in the [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), we don't have to be too concerned. We don't have blue team members trying to stop us. But I do want you to know that when you're using this, maybe you're doing a Pentest and there are blue team members there to know what your traffic looks like. Also, I talked about those flags I like using, an Nmap, the SV flag for version and SC for default scripts. Just know when you run that that you'll see here, if I did a search, TCP contains Nmap, you can see here, I think this is the user agent header, has Nmap scripting engine printed in it. So if you just do a default scan like this, you will be very noisy and Nmap will print it's Nmap scripting engine in the user agent header. And you can see here the protocol is HTTP.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-wireshark?u=76281980&t=282)** So now, it's your turn to use Nmap and Wireshark. Make sure you use it on a host you're authorized to do it, but see what kind of traffic you generate. Figure out how to use filters and also figure out how to change your user agent from Nmap scripting engine to something else. So go out there and figure out how to do that just so you know a little bit more about Nmap and the flags and options you have there. And also, know what your traffic looks like over the wire using Wireshark. So in summary, we should now understand the basic use of Wireshark as both an attacker and defender as well as understand the traffic we generate with Nmap.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/wireshark-and-encrypted-traffic?u=76281980&t=0)** [Wireshark](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Wireshark.md) and Encrypted Traffic. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/smb-enumeration?u=76281980&t=0)** SMB Enumeration. In this lesson, we're going to understand what SMB is used for, as well as demonstrate how to enumerate SMB. So this will start kind of the journey of all the different network services and protocols. And, you know, there's going to be a lot of information about the protocol itself. What I want you to focus on, though, is the hands-on portion of it. So from here, I want you to take the information and learn how to use it hands-on. This is -- [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) is not a memorization test. So just, you know, it's okay if you forget certain things, but I'll try to hint at like flags and switches and things like that and the tools that we'll use, because that's what's important. So SMB is used by [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) as well as [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md). Some people might just focus on the Windows version. There's also a Linux version called Samba. So just because you see SMB open doesn't mean it's a Windows box off the bat. It's also known as common Internet File System CIFS and there's basically three versions. Version 1 is old. On the older machines, you'll find it. On the newer machines like [Windows 10](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Windows%2010.md), it does not come by default, it's disabled. SMB Version 2 is kind of an upgrade from SMB Version 1. Guest access is disabled by default on it, so it's a little bit safer, this SMB Version 1.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/smb-enumeration?u=76281980&t=92)** And then there's SMB Version 3, which is the most secure because guest access is disabled, uses encryption, and you need a username and password. So why is that important? Well, because you want to, as an attacker, be able to log on to SMB and enumerate the different shares within SMB. So there's Port 139 and 445. And as you'll see here in this [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md) scan, we're looking at both. We're looking at 139, which is SMB over net bios and also Port 445 which is SMB over IP address. So in the newer version, you can use an IP address. In the older version on 139, you actually need the hostname. So there's a number of tools already available in Kali to enumerate SMB, and you'll want to keep note of them. Nmap has an Nmap scripting engine script for numerous SMB services and vulnerabilities. There's also a enum for Linux which you can use to enumerate SMB. There's smbclient and rpclient. We'll go over those. So the three common shares are C, which is the C-drive of the remote host. The admin share, which if you have access to this, is great because that's kind of the keys to the kingdom as the admin. And IPC, If you can get onto IPC, you'll notice that you can't actually enumerate any shares. It's not really used for that.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/smb-enumeration?u=76281980&t=187)** Now, the thing is though, if IPC is available to be accessed, you can do things like EternalBlue. But the key points to consider. Can you access SMB as a guest? Do you even need a password? Can you brute-force usernames and passwords? Can you read and write to different shares? Of course, reading is good. Writing is even better because you can put things on two different shares. Now, if you can put things onto a share and you can access that share, you can then execute that script and get a shell and we'll talk about shells later. You actually need to get a shell with something like [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md), which we looked at in the [Wireshark](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Wireshark.md) module. So here, you'll see Nmap an you can use Nmap for a lot of great things. And you can see here you can use an asterisks. If you just do SMB asterisks, it might take a very long time to run all the Nmap scripting engine scripts. So here are some tips. If you want to enumerate, you can do SMB -enum* or vulnerabilities or operating system, different Nmap scripting engines for that. So there's a lot of availability there. #### [NFS enumeration](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/nfs-enumeration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/nfs-enumeration?u=76281980&t=0)** NFS Enumeration. Our learning objectives are to understand what NFS is used for and demonstrate how to enumerate NFS. So NFS stands for Network File System. It was developed back in 1984 by Sun Microsystems. It's kind of like SMB in that it allows you to interact remotely with directories and files on a remote host. You can also mount it to your file system, which I'll demonstrate later. So it's an open standard. Anyone can use it. And we have a bunch of different tools we can use to enumerate this service. #### [SNMP enumeration](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/snmp-enumeration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/snmp-enumeration?u=76281980&t=0)** SNMP Enumeration. Our learning objectives are to understand what SNMP is used for and then demonstrate how to enumerate SNMP. If your mind is going crazy over all these acronyms, I can understand that all these protocols we're learning about, all these different things and me working for the government, I can appreciate and understand why we use acronyms, but we just learned about SMTP and how we're learning about SNMP. They're different, though. So SNMP is simple network management protocol and like its name, it is used for network management. So we can get a whole lot of configuration information and services that are running and users and just a treasure trove of of valuable information as attackers from this protocol. And basically, what this is, it's stored in a database called a MIB or a Master Information Base. And then the MIB further comprised of these long strings of numbers called object identifiers. Now, no one's asking you to memorize specific OIDs. If you could, that's pretty impressive. But certain OIDs refer to certain things like maybe the hostname or usernames or certain services that are running. So a [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) is worth it if you're looking for specific OID strings. SNMP is based on UDP. Some of the other protocols we were looking at were based on TCP, but this is UDP-based and is found on Port 161. In fact, there's a tool look at called 161.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/snmp-enumeration?u=76281980&t=94)** It also uses community strings, the most popular one being public, which is usually read-only. Some of these other strings, like private or secret, you might be able to read and write to it. That's not to say you couldn't read and write to public, but most machines are configured where public is readable only, and sometimes it's a guessing game to figure out which community string is on that machine. Like SMB, there's three versions of SNMP, there's Version 1 and Version 2c, Charlie, which have no encryption, so it's transmitted in the clear, and then version three, which is the most secure because it has encryption. So [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md) is our trusty friend yet again for enumerating SNMP. And you can see here, we're using Nmap SU. I'm the root user so I don't have to use sudo. But if you're the Kali user or a non-root user, you need to sudo with the Su flag on Port 161 and the script that we're using is SNMP Win 32 users. So I'm trying to figure out all the users that are on this machine. Of course, SNMP can enumerate other services that are running. It can do things like netstat and processes and shares, so you can do the SNMP with the asterisks afterwards and possibly get a whole lot of other valuable information from this script. #### [FTP enumeration](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/ftp-enumeration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/ftp-enumeration?u=76281980&t=1)** FTP Enumeration. Our learning objectives are to understand what FTP is used for and to demonstrate how to enumerate FTP. So FTP stands for File Transfer Protocol. And you'll notice with all these protocols they typically describe what they do. Right. So file transfer protocol is to transfer files from one host to another. Now, it's typically found on Port 21 and is a clear text protocol. There is SFTP or secure FTP that uses encryption. It also has the ability for FTP where you may be able to log in anonymously so you can actually -- it will prompt you for a username and you can enter anonymous and then the password can be whatever you want it to be and you have access to that FTP server. The thing though is when you're anonymous, you may not have the same privileges to get and put files onto a server as you would with a normal user or as an admin. So you also notice that if you have, maybe, have a web server on the Internet, that FTP, you can enable FTP so that you can put things like files, [HTML](../../Skills/Web%20Development/HTML.md) or images onto your web server and upload it there. So for FTP scanning, [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md) is our tool yet again and you can see with just our default scan with our SV, SC flags that it will do the ftp_anon script to see if anonymous logins are allowed.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/ftp-enumeration?u=76281980&t=93)** And if they are, it will tell you if that share is readable or writable and also do it as ftp_cest down there. So that's part of the default scripts that Nmap uses just by using the SC flag. We didn't have to use script FTP, although there are some Nmap scripting engine scripts specifically for FTP. So I talked about anonymous login. With Kali, you can just type in FTP and the host and then the port after that, if it's a different port than 21 and you can try to log in. So we'll see here that I logged in as anonymous. I used a password. It used to be that you would enter your email because the clear text protocol, the people that had access to the logs would see what your email was so they knew who you were when you logged in anonymously. Like I said, sometimes when you're the anonymous user, you may not be able to get and put files onto that server like you would if you were, perhaps, an admin or a normal user that had access. So you may want to brute-force your login for FTP. And we can see here, I used [Hydra](../../Glossary/Tool/Hydra.md) again, with little l admin and password is this custom list I used. If you go out there and [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) like what's the default password for FTP, that may give you some some luck and I'll show you that
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/ftp-enumeration?u=76281980&t=188)** in the demo here in a little bit. I also want to point out ascii versus binary mode. And this is something that tripped me up early on in PWK when I was trying to transfer, maybe, executable or binary files from a remote host onto my machine, it defaulted to ascii mode. Ascii mode may be the default when you log in, but that's really used to transfer text files. What you want to switch to is binary mode, if you actually want to download a file. So make sure when you're on the FTP server that if you're downloading anything other than a text file, that you are in binary mode.

It's just being able to get a shell to a remote system and it's a secure shell. So people used to interact with systems, remote systems through Telnet. Well, Telnet was a clear text protocol. And, of course, the first thing that pops up in Telnet, as with SSH, is it asks you for a username and password. So in Telnet, what happened was people would see when people would enter their credentials, they could see everything in the clear so they could filch somebody's username and password. Thus SSH was created to use encryption. And that's why when I talked about Wireshark and seeing clear text on Port 22, that's a red flag to defenders because SSH is encrypted. #### [Protocol tips](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980&t=0)** Protocol Tips. We have one learning objective here, and that's to describe some high level tips when it comes to hacking protocols. So we learned a lot about all these different protocols and services and all the typical ports that we find them on, you know, we know, SSH is Port 22, FTP is Port 21. But if you notice, I also said that I hide Port 22. A lot of other people hide Port 22 from would-be attackers so that it's not on a well-known port. As we saw in the FTP lab, I didn't have FTP running on Port 21. I had it running on port 2121, which will also see later. So just because a port is on a particular -- or a service on a particular port or we see a particular port open, I should say, don't assume that's what that service is. HTTP has a whole bunch of different ports. If we know ACP, it might be on 80, it might be on 8080, it might be on 8088. So that's to say let's use our tools, let's use [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md), let's scan every single port. Because if you're thinking like someone who's making [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), you know how these tools work. You know how Nmap works. You know what typical ports Nmap scans. Why wouldn't you want to be clever and crafty and hide your whatever service on a port that you know Nmap is not going to be able to scan. So that's why I like to scan every single port because you can never assume that just because Nmap does a default port scan of
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980&t=98)** well-known ports, that is going to find all the different open ports when it's test day. So that's why we do the SV flag and the SC flag and Nmap because I like to know what the version is. And we saw even with Nmap when we looked at the SMTP demo, it didn't even recognize the service. We didn't know the service until we used [Ncat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) and we manually enumerated it. So that's where you're starting to build your skills. You're starting to say, well, if Nmap doesn't work for me, maybe I can go to Ncat or [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) or maybe Masscan is the right tool here. So it's knowing which tools to use when things fail you. The best hackers are able to manually enumerate things and don't rely on a particular tool. They have a bunch of tools in their tool belt like Batman. All right. Also, keep these things in mind. Can you log in without credentials? We saw SMB with these null sessions or guest sessions where, you know, it prompts you for a password, you just hit "Enter" and you have access to shares. Also, FTP with anonymous login, you just enter anonymous and you log in. What do you have access to? And that's where you need to think about. And that's why I showed you that enumeration with Nmap and all the other tools. Can you not only read something, can you also write? Can you pull down files, can you upload files, can you download files? The other thing you'll realize is if you have access to the entire machine,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980&t=190)** it's a little bit like information overload. Where do I start looking? What is out of place? And that's why I stress that it's important to do labs. It's important to keep doing things like Hack The Box and CTFs over and over again because the more boxes you enumerate, the more you'll realize like, hey, that's not a particular service I've seen. Normally, maybe that's, you know, maybe there's a vulnerability there, or I know which particular files to look at in [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) to tell me more about the configuration of that machine. And it just comes with more and more reps. So don't assume that, you know, because you're new to this that you're not going to be good at it. It's just it takes a long time to do it. That's why people spend months preparing for OSCP because you need to get those reps down and become familiar with how boxes are laid out. Also, can you brute-force logins? We saw, you know, we can assume maybe there's a root user on Linux. Maybe we can find the password for that. You know, even with FTP, even with a [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) search, we found that the default credentials for admin is password and that worked for us. You know, maybe we don't even have to brute force. Maybe it's just some googling and you find what the default credentials are for a particular service. And like I mentioned before, you know, figure out what you can do when you log in. We saw the anonymous user and FTP couldn't put things on the server, but we saw that when we logged in with the admin user, we could put things on the server.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980&t=283)** So figure out what you can do once you log in to these services. Also, use your hacker mindset. Think around the problem. If you keep hitting a wall, stop hitting your head against the wall and think laterally. Think with agility. So use your hacker mindset. Just because a protocol doesn't let you execute a file, where else can you put it? And that's why you saw when I put that ASP Shell on that machine, I couldn't execute it directly from FTP, but I could put it in the web route because I knew where the web route was and luckily we had access to it. So think like that. Just because you can't execute something with a particular service, what other vulnerabilities are there? And we can chain vulnerabilities together. And that's why it's important to pay attention when we do web app attacks, because, for example, if we can't put something in the web route, but we could put it in the temporary directory in temp where there's globally readable, writable executable permissions. If there's local and file inclusion, that means you can go all the way back from the browser to the temporary directory and that's where you can execute your Shell from. So that's what makes very good hackers. Are people who can think like that, who think, well, you know, I know that this service, I can't execute something, but what other vulnerabilities can I look at and look for that will help me get a Shell on this machine?
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/protocol-tips?u=76281980&t=377)** So in summary, now hopefully, you understand some high-level tips when it comes to hacking protocols. When we get to the lab, relax. You know, you're not supposed to get a Shell on this lab. It's just a familiarization lab. Okay. So just become familiar with the different protocols, interact with the different protocols. And like I said, it just takes reps of doing this over and over and over again. So no pressure on the lab. Just enjoy it.

### 4. Web Application Penetration Testing

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Web application enumeration](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=0)** Web application [Penetration Testing](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Penetration%20Testing.md). Web application enumeration. Our learning objectives are to understand what tools are available to enumerate [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md), describe the limitations of these scanners, know what directory brute-forcing is and how it can aid in website enumeration, and explain why manual enumeration of web applications is important. Do you recognize that picture? It's of our friend [WordPress](../../Skills/Web%20Development/WordPress.md), which we'll get a lot more familiar with in the upcoming labs. In full disclosure, when I saw a web server in PWK or [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md), I got nervous because our attack service just grew exponentially. There could be various pages in there, there could be admin accounts, there could be user accounts. Maybe I have to register for something to gain access to it. It introduces a whole level of complexity in a larger attack surface. Now, this is my bread and butter and really it's just a matter of breaking things down into smaller pieces and making it more digestible. And, again, the more reps you do, the more experience you have with web applications, the less scary it becomes. You'll also notice that there's all these [content management systems](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Content%20Management%20Systems%20(CMS).md), there's WordPress, there's [Drupal](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Drupal.md), there's Joomla. And what makes them interesting is they have a large attack surface as well. Just because so many people use them. You'll notice in the news it will say, you know, this plugin for WordPress is vulnerable and now five million sites are now vulnerable to a unrestricted file upload bypass vulnerability. So because so many people use this,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=94)** so many people are looking for zero days in these applications to exploit them. So it makes it a larger attack surface there. Also, if you're doing bug bounties now or if you're a [Web Application Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Web%20Application%20Security.md) engineer or pen tester, your mindset needs to change. Doesn't matter if the secure flag isn't set on a cookie. It doesn't matter if there's a CRSF attack if there -- because it's a client side attack, if no one's there to be exploited. As I said, we should focus on server-side attacks. I know there's client-side attacks like cross-site scripting and CRSF but really we want to focus on the server-side attack. So get out of the bug bounty mindset of finding specific vulnerabilities in a web application. If you want to do that, I recommend the EWPT exam because that is very specific to web applications. So enumeration with our friend [Nmap](../../Glossary/Tool/Nmap.md) yet again. So keep in mind when you're doing your scan with Nmap, that's probably the first time you're going to observe a web server and don't assume it's on Port 80 or 443. It can also be on ports like 8080 or 8443, but also understand if it's HTTP or HTTPS. Seen a lot of junior pen testers try a port using HTTP when they should be using HTTPS different protocol and they can't connect to the site. So play around with those protocols on those different ports and see if maybe it changes depending on it's
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=191)** HTTP or HTTPS. We can also remind our -- refine our Nmap scan with that vuln scan. I talked about the vuln scan with Nmap in the very beginning. That kind of has a very robust output. It's not as good as things like [Burp Suite](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Burp%20Suite.md), active scan. Of course, you can't use Burp Suite Pro in OSCP, but it's kind of, you know, it's a fast scanner -- vulnerability scanner for web applications. Also, we should get an output in Nmap of the version of Apache or Nginx or IIS. Is that version vulnerable? So check that out at first when you start enumerating these web servers. You'll see here is the output from an Nmap scan and it gives us with the vuln script in it. You'll see it already gives us a lot of good information and I was making fun of the HTTP flag not set with cookies, but here you go, right here. Or CRSF. Here you go again. Also, let's look at the bottom. We can see a possible [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) injection that's great for us. And maybe enumerating users or usernames and passwords or maybe even getting a Shell in the box. So we're also trying to look for different folders and directories on the web server. With [Nikto](../../Glossary/Tool/Nikto.md), that also does stuff like that. This is a great scanner. Chris Sulo is a friend and I've spoken to him a lot about Nikto and how he saved me with OSCP. There's a lot of output with Nikto, and there are also a lot of false positives,
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=286)** but test everything because the one thing in there that's buried in that output might be what gets you a Shell in OSCP. I'm just saying. But, you know, again, this Nikto I think is a great tool to use on web servers. If it's not on Port 80, you have to specify the port of it's 443 or 8080. So keep that in mind. Directory brute-forcing. Why do we do directory brute-forcing? Well, if we use a tool like Burp Suite, which we'll talk about later, that's not brute-forcing directories for you. It's not figuring out what directories there are. We have to use tools for that. And if you're not brute-forcing directories, you may miss out on an admin directory or some juicy directory that might have information that will help us enumerate the server or vulnerabilities. So it always suggest running a brute force against a web server to see what is on it. It also finds things through response codes, and we'll talk about response codes as well. But it will look at a response code of like 200, meaning that you can reach that web page or 404, meaning that that page doesn't exist and it will tell you whether the page exists or not. Whether it gets a 200, it will -- you'll get output or 404, it won't give you output. It doesn't find vulnerabilities. A directory may have a vulnerability in it, but a directory brute force is not going to say, hey, look at this. This is what's vulnerable. [Dirb](../../Glossary/Tool/Dirb.md) is a fast and dirty directory brute force.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=380)** I really like it. It goes really, really fast. And as you can see here, it gives you the response codes. It tells you whether there's a 200 or 403. And it also tells you if a directory is listable, if you can look at every single file in that directory. So I really like running this first because the other two brute forces take a lot more time. It's default installed in Kali. You can specify a [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) list, it has a default word list in it, but there's a word list I like using and we'll look at Dirbuster. And you can see here I like using that directory-list-2.3-medium.txt. So I always like using no matter whether it's Dirbuster or [Gobuster](../../Glossary/Tool/Gobuster.md), which we'll look at next. But Dirbusters [OWASP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OWASP.md) directory brute forcer is written in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md). You can specify which word list you want to use. You can specify the file extension. That's why it's important to enumerate the technology, whether the server is using [PHP](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PHP.md) or ASP, whether it has text files on it, whether it's using Perl, maybe there's CGI in there. So I would definitely recommend knowing what file extensions to specify or trying a few different ones. It's also already installed in Kali. The one that's not is Gobuster and Gobuster is probably my favorite. So Gobuster is also a command line tool. It goes very fast. As you can see here, I'm specifying that word list again. Actually, I'm specifying a different one, but my favorite is that medium word list.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=475)** So you can see here you can also specify extensions. I have PHP, TXT, [HTML](../../Skills/Web%20Development/HTML.md), and it's giving me the response code of the server as well. It's written in Go. I really, really like this. Download it and give it a try. Also, I had to use Wappalyzer or get that extension because that also enumerates website technology very quickly. From this, you can already tell it's WordPress. You can already tell there's a [MySQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/MySQL.md) database there. It doesn't show the version of Apache, but we know we're working with Apache. So I really like using Wappalyzer. Also, don't discount manual enumeration. Tools are great, but know how to use manual enumeration to figure out what's going on. Always look at the robots.txt file, what it says is disallowed. We really -- that's the juicy information to us as hackers. Right. It's disallowed. We want to look at it. Also, view the source of the page. Right-click "View source." Maybe there's some meta tags, maybe there's some comments in there. Maybe the developer left comments like having to build out this directory, but not yet. But yet we can surf to that directory. Or maybe something's grayed out on the page, but it's, you know, it's in the source of the page like a certain directory and we can still surf to it. So always look at the source of the page, which you can also do using cURL. If you use the cURL command, you're just looking at the source of the page and you may see things you may miss when you're looking at the beautiful browser. Also, look at cookies. If there's a PHPSESSID, you can be pretty sure
>
> **[9:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/web-application-enumeration?u=76281980&t=568)** it's running PHP. Also, look at the network tab, refresh the page, look at all the connections that website makes. Maybe it will give us other directories and other files we should be looking at. Burp Suite. If you're anywhere in the [Web Application Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Web%20Application%20Security.md) space, you should know what Burp Suite is. It is by far hands down the most popular intercepting proxy out there. It's made by PortSwigger in the UK. The other thing is, if you've used this before and you type in a web page and hit "Enter" and you see nothing happens, you know, that's because you forgot to turn intercept off or from on to off, I should say. It's on by default. So that means it's just going to sit there and it's just going to wait for you to turn off the proxy. So ensure that when you start a Burp Suite, you turn intercept to off. It's called intercepting proxy because you can modify the packets. We saw [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) and we changed -- we downgraded HTTP to 1.0. Well, you can do that a lot easier using an intercepting proxy. You can change things from GET requests to POST requests. GET request, meaning you are getting a website, POST request, meaning you're posting data, like you're entering a username and password. So you can do that easily here using Burp Suite. So repeater. I really like repeater because you can easily analyze different types of requests. You can see a POST request here. I can easily change that into a GET request by changing
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-intercepting-proxies-part-1?u=76281980&t=288)** the request method. I can easily analyze the response. Maybe I want to change the password to something else and I can quickly do that here as opposed to going through the browser. If I want to test a [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) injection, I can quickly do that here as opposed to having to look at the browser over and over and over every time I send a different request. So repeater allows you to send different types of requests and analyze the response. Intruder. Intruder takes a little bit of getting used to. It's used for brute-forcing logins. You can fuzz web applications. You'll notice that it's throttled in the community edition. So if you're going to do things like brute-forcing username and password, [Hydra](../../Glossary/Tool/Hydra.md) might be a better tool for you to use. You can grep specific strings. So I'll show you the different types of payloads. I typically use sniper, so I'll typically use one value or change one value in the requests and that usually works well for me. You can use battering ram, which places the same payload value in all the positions as opposed to just one. You can use pitchfork or you can use cluster bomb. I don't talk about this here, but if you use something like Shellshock, cluster bomb might be a great place for that or even battering ram as well.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/sql-injections?u=76281980&t=0)** [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) injections. Learning objectives are to understand how to identify SQL injection vulnerabilities and demonstrate how to manually exploit injections. So SQL, Structured Query Language in [Relational Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Relational%20Databases.md). [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) are everywhere in web applications. If you've ever been shopping for something on Amazon and there's a price and there's an item, there's probably an underlying database for that. If you've ever signed up for a forum, you know, if you sign up for PWK, you have access to the offensive security forum and you have a username and password and you interact with other users in that forum, there's probably an underlying database there that's storing that information. And because there's so many databases out there in web applications, that the -- a SQL injection attack has been around for a very, very long time. A SQL injection is basically being able to inject our own SQL statements -- raw SQL statements into [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md) or the URL itself, and that allows us to query the database, interact with the database, delete things, add things, and do very bad things like figure out usernames and passwords or other sensitive information in these databases. It's also important to know what the underlying database is. Is it [MySQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/MySQL.md), is it MSSQL, is it Oracle DB? Why is that important? Because the way you structure your queries is going to depend upon the underlying database.

#### [Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/cross-site-scripting-xss?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/cross-site-scripting-xss?u=76281980&t=1)** Cross-Site Scripting. Our learning objectives are to understand the basics of cross-site scripting, demonstrate how to use the BeEF framework, and identify the different types of cross-site scripting vulnerabilities. So I've spoken a lot about server-side attacks attacking FTP servers, attacking [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md), attacking SMB. So these are all server-side attacks. Client-side attacks are having to have someone interact like a victim, open up their browser and surf to your website. We saw this client-side attack in the [Wireshark](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Wireshark.md) module and the drive by download, that person had to click that link and be enticed to go to our website. Cross-site scripting is an injection attack, so we're actually allowed to or were able to inject [JavaScript](../../Skills/Software%20Development/JavaScript.md) into a web form or into the URL bar itself, kind of like [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) injection where we could also write SQL queries into things like into the URL bar itself or into a form. Cross-site scripting, you can write JavaScript into the URL or into a form. And what makes that dangerous is JavaScript allows attackers to do things like steal cookies, to redirect victims to their controlled page. And you can also inject things like [HTML](../../Skills/Web%20Development/HTML.md), which is not as bad. Why?
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/cross-site-scripting-xss?u=76281980&t=91)** Because HTML is static, so an HTML injection would just be adding things like, you know, click this link and that person on the page could see a click this link, but it wouldn't actually execute any kind of script. So there's three types of JavaScript -- or I'm sorry -- cross-site scripting attacks. One is reflected, which you send somebody a link and it executes the JavaScript when they click it. They're stored where it's actually written to the server, it's actually written to the page. Reflect is also written on the page when it's executed, but stored is on the page. So you'll see this a lot in web [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md) or forums, I should say, where people visit the forum and there's stored JavaScript in it that may redirect the victim to the attacker's controlled page. Or there might be a small little image in there that actually is redirecting someone's browser or having their browser contact the attacker's controlled server so they can steal cookies and then log in as the victim. And there's also DOM-Based. DOM-Based is hard to understand for a lot of people, but it's not written to the server like stored or reflected where you can actually see it on the source of the page. DOM occurs in the [document object model](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Document%20Object%20Model%20(DOM).md) of the page or document object model, which is kind of a hard thing to understand within itself. But we'll talk about that a little bit later. So it doesn't -- it's not dealing with the server itself.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/cross-site-scripting-xss?u=76281980&t=187)** It's dealing with the DOM, which is an interface in the browser. So you won't actually see it written to the page. All right. Reflected. Reflected means a victim has to click on a link and within that link is malicious JavaScript. So it's very easy to see that if you click a link that has a script tag in it, that JavaScript will be executed. So what an attacker may do is create something like a Bitly link or a shortened link that a victim clicks or they may create a typo squatted domain. What is typo squatted mean? Typo squatted means if I want a typo squat something like [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) instead of O's, I could put zeros and the victim would look at that link and say, oh, I'm going to Google. But in fact, it's Google with two zeros as opposed to two O's. So if the victim clicks the link, you can have a script in there, you can write JavaScript that redirects them to your controlled page, which could look like a Gmail login and is actually the attacker's controlled site. And the victim enters their credentials and the attacker steals their credentials from there. You'll see this a lot in bug bounty. There are so many cross-site -- reflected cross-site scripting vulnerabilities, and I see this a lot with payloads where they'll do this script, alert one script, and they'll show the big -- I call it the big scary "1." As a client,

Okay. You can make a big scary "1" pop-up. Let me show you what it looks like. Wow. There it is. So here's the vulnerable site. As you can see, it's MyBB. It's a vulnerability in MyBB. But that's a forward slash that percent to F, script and it's writing an alert box with one in it. We can do better than that. And I'll show you that with the BeEF framework. So if you analyze the source, if you're having issues with your payload, you can kind of mess around and look at the source of the page and see how it's written into the source of the page to modify your payload. I've had to do this a lot with submitting cross-site scripting vulnerabilities for different programs where you're trying to get your cross-site scripting payload to work and you need to focus on the parentheses and where to end. You'll see input and then you'll see that I put the finishing greater than sign and that closed it. And then I added my script tag and that's why that worked as a payload there.
