#### [File upload vulnerabilities](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/file-upload-vulnerabilities?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/file-upload-vulnerabilities?u=76281980&t=0)** File Upload vulnerabilities. Our learning objectives are to understand what file upload vulnerabilities are and identify how to exploit file upload vulnerabilities. So a lot of applications allow people to upload different kinds of files. Maybe it's an avatar image, maybe it's a medical application where you can upload receipts in a PDF form. A lot of them you have to be authenticated, some you don't have to be authenticated, which is even better for us as attackers. But what this allows us to do is possibly change the file extension to then upload a malicious file. And again, it's important to understand the underlying architecture of the server. What -- is it [PHP](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PHP.md), is it ASP? That's going to ultimately determine what type of file you're going to upload. So our administrator portal for the Awesome Photoblog doesn't allow file extensions named PHP. Why doesn't it do that? Because it runs PHP, and if I can upload a shell, it will execute that shell and I'm able to get on that server. So it really depends how the code is written. Some have regex, so it's looking for different types of file extensions. In this case, it's actually looking for .php, so I could fool it by making it php3 or making it an uppercase P lowercase h uppercase P again to block that regex.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/file-upload-vulnerabilities?u=76281980&t=99)** So it's not a very well-thought out application. Of course, it's intentionally vulnerable, but here that's how I tricked it. You can also do other things like phtml as well. I have a whole other slide to show you what are the things you can try. So if you understand the underlying architecture of the server and you know it's running PHP, here are a few extensions that you can use. ASP, you can use asp or aspx. Perl, you have a few other options to choose from. JSP and Coldfusion. So just because one file extension type is blocked doesn't mean another file type is not blocked, which is allowed. So try these because I've done a few hack the box challenges or CTFs where PHP is blocked, but php5 is not. So this is a good source to take a look at as well. Mime-types. Why are mime-types important? Because some servers don't look at the extension itself, they look at the mime-type of the file, so you can run the file command and mime-type and you can see here that it is text/x-php and I changed the file extension to jpg to a jpeg and run mime-type again. Well, it doesn't fool it. It sees that it's a PHP file. So in this case simply changing the extension will not trick the server into thinking that is actually a JPEG file.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/file-upload-vulnerabilities?u=76281980&t=195)** So what do you do? You use [Burp Suite](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Burp%20Suite.md). So when you intercept this post request in Burp Suite you are changing the content type from application x-php to image/jpeg. So now it thinks it's a JPEG file and not a PHP file despite having a PHP extension. You can do this by hiding code in an image. I thought this was a pretty cool little script from techyzilla. And here's the script that I used for -- to basically take a JPEG file and obfuscate PHP into that. And you can see here I run the strings command on it and you can see my little shell down there written in PHP. So if I run mime-type on this, even though it has a php3 extension, the mime-type is jpeg. That has to do with magic bytes. You can you can look this up in [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md), but you see magic bytes JFIF. If you look up magic bytes for things like GIF or GIF files, I know there's a big war about what you call it, or any type of file, there are magic bytes. So when something looks at a file, it will look at things like the headers of the file, and if it says JFIF, it will think it's a -- well, in this case, it thinks it's a JPEG file.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/file-upload-vulnerabilities?u=76281980&t=289)** But these magic bytes are also important as well. So it's recognizing this file from the magic bytes as a JPEG file. So here we go. I was able to obfuscate my code in that image and I ran [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) and was able to get a reverse shell by tricking the server into thinking that my PHP -- that the PHP JPEG was a JPEG when, in fact, it had PHP embedded inside of it and the server, of course, is running PHP. So that's how I was able to get a reverse shell. So in summary, we should now understand what file upload vulnerabilities are and identify how to exploit file upload vulnerabilities.

#### [XXE attacks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/xxe-attacks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/xxe-attacks?u=76281980&t=1)** XXE Attacks. Our learning objectives are to understand what XML External Entities(XXE) injection attacks are and identify how to exploit XXE. External Entities is a mouthful, so I'm glad they've abbreviated it here with an acronym. So what is XXE? It's a vulnerability that allows an attacker to interfere with the way an application works. It parses XML. So in this case, it allows us to view files on that server. In some cases, it allows us to execute commands, and it also allows us to do things like, reach out to our own server. So when you see XML data being parsed, when you can see the underlying architecture of the server, it then allows us to try to figure out, well, can we leverage this? If I see something, this is XML in here, maybe this is something that I can leverage into an attack. So like I said, it can leverage SSRF which stands for server-side request forgery, meaning that you're using that server to either enumerate itself in timing attacks. Maybe you can figure out what ports are open or also enumerate the internal network it's on to figure out if other hosts are up on that internal network so we can pivot into that network, leveraging XXE. And like I said, XXE has become very popular recently.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/xxe-attacks?u=76281980&t=95)** It's not in the PWK material that I've seen, but it is in the AWAE, the Offensive Security Advanced Web Attack course, and I've seen it in production environment. So applications that parse XML, things like SOAP or simple object access protocol, applications that use that. So here we have an application and we have to figure out the structure of XML. XML is not like [HTML](../../Skills/Web%20Development/HTML.md), hypertext markup language. They're both markup languages, but HTML has tags like body tags or an HTML tag or things that we know, title, tags that are defined. XML has its own. You can name it anything like this, you can name it anything right here. You can name the tags, anything. So you have to know what the tags are in order to inject our payload into it. So here we have a user and a password tag. So we're going to take these tags and we're going to try to inject our own syntax into it. So what do I mean by that? #### [Content management systems](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/content-management-systems?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/content-management-systems?u=76281980&t=1)** [Content Management Systems](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Content%20Management%20Systems%20(CMS).md). Our learning objectives are to understand what Content Management Systems, CMS' are, and why people use them, as well as identify how to enumerate and exploit a CMS. If you've been on the Internet, you've run into a CMS. So when I was an agent, I was trying to figure out how to build websites and I went to school for building websites with [HTML](../../Skills/Web%20Development/HTML.md) and CSS and [JavaScript](../../Skills/Software%20Development/JavaScript.md) and basically learned how to build websites from the ground up. And it's hard. It's very hard. So why do we have Content Management Systems? We have them because it's a user-friendly way to build a website. If you have an [E-Commerce](../../Skills/Web%20Development/E-Commerce.md) site or if you're a small business or even a large business that wants to stand up a website quickly and in a user-friendly way and have multiple people have access to be able to manage that content within that site, a CMS is the way to go and that's why you see them all over the place. So it typically has a dashboard that a user or an administrator can log into and deal with things like blogs and plugins and themes and make it look nice and add plugins to it. So it's really -- because it's so user-friendly, so many people use them, making it such a big target for people to find zero days in them because if everyone's using the same technology across
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/content-management-systems?u=76281980&t=94)** the Internet, it's profitable for an attacker to find a vulnerability in that [Content Management](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Content%20Management.md) System because then you could exploit multiple sites, hundreds of sites, thousands of sites. If you see the news about [WordPress](../../Skills/Web%20Development/WordPress.md) and finding a vulnerable plugin, it says it affects 20,000 users+. > **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/fuzzing-the-application?u=76281980&t=330)** Offset. Our length is 1,024 -u of where we crashed the program, 39654138. And we see there's an exact match of 146, which was around that 150 mark where we saw the program crash from our fuzzer. So what we want to do now is be sure that the offset is 146. So I've created this other program and you may have seen me launch this earlier. But here's our offset. We have A times 146, which is right here, our exact match at 146. Now, I'm going to send 4 B's and I'm going to send 90 C's. You could make that less than that if you wanted. But the important thing is seeing if our 4 B's land and overwrite EIP to make sure that we've controlled EIP. So we'll go back. Like I said, a lot of back and forth here.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/fuzzing-the-application?u=76281980&t=409)** It will start this again.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/fuzzing-the-application?u=76281980&t=422)** And now, we will send our offset script and see if we've overwritten EIP with those B's, which should be Hex character 42. And we sent four of them. So going back, we see EIP was overwritten by our 4 B's. 42424242. So now, we know that our offset is correct.
>
> **[7:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/fuzzing-the-application?u=76281980&t=461)** So now, we should understand how we can cause a vulnerable application to crash. You saw me do that with the Fuzzer. You saw how we can use Metasploit to do that pattern create. So it creates a unique pattern of characters. Then we can send that payload and crash the application and from that, determine from EIP, where it crashed exactly by using pattern offset and then sending another payload where we not only send the exact amount of A's, but 4 B's and C's after that to ensure that we're cleanly overwriting EIP. In summary, we should now understand how to fuzz an application and find the offset where EIP is overwritten.

So what I mean by this, ultimately, we want to write shell code so that when we do our buffer overflow, the program executes into or jumps into our shell code. #### [Using Metasploit or not](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-metasploit-or-not?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-metasploit-or-not?u=76281980&t=0)** Using [Metasploit](../../Glossary/Framework/Metasploit.md) in the [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) or Not. Our learning objective is to determine the pluses and minuses of using Metasploit on the OSCP. So this is a question everyone has to ask themselves. During the OSCP, should I use Metasploit? And there's a lot of factors that go into that and it's probably something you should start to think about before you are in the exam. So when you're in the environment, the lab environment for PWK and you look at the forums, many people obviously say, don't use Metasploit. You may see old boxes with exploits like EternalBlue. So what I would do is I would find a non-metasploit way to exploit the EternalBlue vulnerability and go from there because, again, when you're not relying on Metasploit, you're reading code and modifying code and having to take additional steps, which I think will translate nicely into OSCP day when, you know, when you're trying to find the correct exploit code and you have some comfort in knowing well, in the labs, I've done them without Metasploit. But also do them both ways. Use Metasploit and don't use Metasploit. And I'll use that with things like [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) map. I'll use SQL map in labs and then I won't and I'll try to determine how much harder it is not using SQL map than when I do use SQL map. Same thing with Metasploit. I try to determine, okay, I can use Metasploit,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-metasploit-or-not?u=76281980&t=94)** but what if I don't? And that's why I think going through the labs and the examples that I've given, some Metasploit modules are very simple. They're simply uploading a file with a cURL command and you could easily do that yourself. So when you have some comfort in looking at exploit code and knowing what an exploit does, you understand the complexity. If there's hashing [Algorithms](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Algorithms.md) that go towards it or trying to brute force dates and times and things like that, it gets more complex. And then you should start thinking, well, maybe Metasploit is the right way to go here because, you know, maybe it's difficult for me to try to figure out all this on my own. And, again, I think the worst feeling is deciding to use Metasploit and it not working. So I'd say go with your gut and if you think that that exploit is the correct one in Metasploit and it doesn't work, again, change up your payloads, look at the targets. You know, maybe there's something in there. Maybe you didn't configure it correctly, maybe you didn't set your LHOST or rhosts or whatever it may be. Maybe you didn't do that right. So double, triple-check whether you did it right or not. And, maybe, you've lost the box somehow and you just need to simply need to reset it. So don't use Metasploit as a crutch. If you go through the PWK labs and you use Metasploit on every box, if you could, in theory, I don't know if you can, but if you use Metasploit in every box, you're really going to do yourself a disservice because when it comes time to test it and you can only use it once,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-metasploit-or-not?u=76281980&t=187)** you're really not going to know, you know, how to use Metasploit very well, but you won't know how to look at code, analyze it. And again, that's what differentiates you from a script kiddie, someone who just goes into Metasploit and selects the correct module and then just hits, "Exploit" or "Run." You know, the differentiator is the fact that you can look at code and you can become familiar with figuring out what it does. And, of course, that takes a lot of googling and a lot of enumeration figuring it out. So like I said, try both ways, try using Metasploit, try not using Metasploit and have that comfort and that self-confidence that you're able to do things not relying on Metasploit. But in the end, the choice is yours. I think in my OSCP attempts, I used Metasploit. I believe I used it every single time, but it was typically towards the end of the test. My fear was using Metasploit too early and then not being able to use it again. So weigh the cost-benefit analysis. If I'm only two hours into OSCP and I think I found a Metasploit module, should I use it now? Your answer might be yes. Your answer might be, I found some exploit, I've tried to find the code, all I can find is a Metasploit module. I don't know how to write Ruby and I don't feel comfortable doing this on my own. So I am going to try Metasploit now. So it's really a case-by-case basis. But in my strategy, in my mind, it was always something that I should save for the end
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/using-metasploit-or-not?u=76281980&t=281)** when all else failed. I'll just try Metasploit and see if it works. And also know that, you know, if you're thinking of Offensive Security, who makes this? Do I make a box that is only exploitable with Metasploit? You know, do I make somebody who's taking this test have to use it? So think of it that way as well. I try to put myself in the mind of the test makers, just like someone who's taking the test is would they write something? Would they make a box only vulnerable to a Metasploit module? And are there other ways to exploit it? Is there other public exploit code out there available for somebody to find it? And, maybe, there is, and maybe it's just a matter of, you know, maybe it's not an [Exploit-DB](../../Glossary/Service/Exploit-DB.md) but it's in [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md), someone else who has the exploit code and you don't need to use Metasploit. So that's why we always go back and you always hear enumerate, enumerate, enumerate. That goes the same for your [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Searches or Bing Searches or whatever search engine you want to use is trying to find that exploit code and not having to go to Metasploit. So here's our quiz. Which Metasploit module can you use on more than one target? I said this in the last lesson and you should know it. I preface this by saying, check the exam guide from the time that this is recorded and the time that you take the OSCP, maybe things have changed, so go and check now. But the answer to this is going to be, multi-handler, which you've seen me use a few times. But, again, go to the Offensive Security website, look at the exam guide and ensure that this information is up-to-date. So in summary, you should now be able to determine the pluses and minuses of using Metasploit on the OSCP.

#### [The different types of shells](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/the-different-types-of-shells?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/the-different-types-of-shells?u=76281980&t=0)** Module 8: Getting a Shell, Now What? The different types of shells. Our learning objectives are to understand the difference between a bind shell and a reverse shell, explain the difference between a good shell and a bad shell, and know why architecture matters when it comes to shells. So you've seen me get some shells on some boxes, finding some exploits and getting shells. What happens next is typically in [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) land or PWK is we need to escalate privileges further to become root or system if it's a [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) box. In order to do that, we need to have a stable shell. In order to do things and interact with the machine, we need to have a stable shell. You'll find some shells are unstable or they die. And that's because of different things. Sometimes the architecture of the box is different than the shell that we have. Sometimes we're not calling the shell correctly, or we're trying to use a bash shell, and the [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) machine doesn't even have bash. They have SH, they have a shell, and we don't know that. And we need to kind of figure out what is on that box that we can actually get a foothold on it and also how we can exploit privileges or escalate privileges, I should say, which does in some cases require that we're able to sudo if it's a Linux box or that the architecture matters. I was talking about that with [Metasploit](../../Glossary/Framework/Metasploit.md) is the architecture really matters. So you'll find that if you don't have a good shell, your functionality is greatly reduced. So what's the difference between a bind shell and a reverse shell?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/the-different-types-of-shells?u=76281980&t=97)** A bind shell is good when the victim doesn't have a firewall up. So basically, the victim will open up a port, a listener port. So we've used some kind of exploit and we have the victim open up a port -- a listener port, and we attach ourselves to that using Meterpreter or [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) or [Ncat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md). And that's how we connect to the box with a direct connection from us as an attacker directly to that open port on the victim machine. If there's a firewall up, that makes it a whole lot harder because a firewall will block all incoming traffic except from well-known ports, right? From ports like 80 or 483. They're expecting web traffic and things like that. A reverse shell on the other hand is a lot safer because what happens is we're having the victim reach out to our machine. So we create a listener locally on our box and the victim will attach itself to us. So this is a lot more useful when a victim has a firewall up. But I've seen many cases where a bind shell will work and is more stable than a reverse shell. So that's why I say if a payload fails with the Meterpreter, maybe you need to change it up. Maybe you need to make the TCP reverse shell a bind shell. But typically, your reverse shell is going to be a lot more effective than a bind shell because most machines now have a firewall up. So you'll see here this is the example that the victim doesn't have a firewall and the bind shell works fine with
>

So I did this locally in my lab. I took the firewall down the bind shell worked fine. I put the firewall up and it failed. So you'll see on the left it says, "Exploit completed, no session was created" because the firewall is up. But using a reverse shell, and you'll see the payload on the right side windows/meterpreter/reverse_tcp, we have a session open up because the victim machine is connecting to us through the firewall and they're able to do that with this reverse shell. So how do we -- aside from Meterpreter, how do we figure out how to make a reverse shell work? Because reverse shells are our preference. Right. #### [How to upgrade a shell](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980&t=0)** How to Upgrade a Shell. Our learning objectives are to understand why we try to upgrade a shell and demonstrate the various ways to upgrade a shell. So I hope the last lesson didn't get you down. There are ways to go from a bad shell to a good shell, and I'm going to tell you how you can do that. We're hackers, right? We're going to think outside the box. If we're stuck with a bad shell, there has to be a good way to get a good shell. Some of those ways are in Meterpreter. You can go from a command shell and you'll see here I used multi-managed shell Meterpreter. And again, this is going to be something that more in PWKs and CTFs than actually [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) because again, this is a module here. So you will migrate from a command shell which has limited functionality to a Meterpreter shell where we can do things like set up a pivot point. So that's to say that when you are in CTFs and PWK and you're using [Metasploit](../../Glossary/Framework/Metasploit.md) and you do get a command shell off the bat, there is a module that you can go from getting just a command shell to a Meterpreter shell with a lot more functionality. In some cases, you'll find you have a restricted shell. So on the top image or SSH image, which I did say is great to get an SSH session into a box. But you'll see here we're in a restricted rbash shell, so we're trying to change directory to root says, "Restricted whoami" It's not letting us specify a forward slash. You can get around that.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980&t=93)** And there are some -- I've seen some CTFs or boxes that are like tailored around escapes escaping these types of restricted shells. One way to do that is, you know, I've taught you -t and you can do a bin bash or bin shell, bin sh. In some cases, you can do bash --noprofile. And in this case, it worked for me. And you can see now I can change directory to etc and /etc/passwd. So I have a whole lot more functionality than I did in this rbash shell. That's how to escape in rbash shell is to try this -t bash noprofile. Sometimes it works, sometimes it doesn't. But this is a tool to put in your toolbar. Also here, you'll see that I have a [PHP](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PHP.md) shell and you can see I'm issuing commands and I don't get any output. I don't see anything. So what I want to do is I want to upgrade to a Meterpreter shell possible multi-handler, like I said, multi-handler when I recorded this -- when I'm recording this now, you can use multi-handler on any box you want. So that's what I typically do is once I have a PHP shell, normally through a web shell or I get RCE through a web server is I'll get on the box with the [Netcat](../../Glossary/Tool/Netcat.md) shell and then I will download. You'll see curl wget, which you should recognize as ways to get the shell from my box, and I'm making it executable. And ultimately, I am executing this shell.elf, which is something that I created with [msfvenom](../../Glossary/Framework/Metasploit.md).
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980&t=189)** So if you're on a [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) box, you can use msfvenom to create this elf file and ultimately have it execute. And you see here now I have a nice Meterpreter session open. So here's a cheat sheet. If you do get on a box and you do see have limited functionality, you can do this [python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md) import pty. That's importing -- basically having Python use this library and this language to create its own shell, which is very stable, I found. It is echo os.system bin bash or even bin shell and some cases and bin sh -i. So give those a try if you don't see anything. If you're on the box and you don't see any type of prompt, give each of those a try. Also, note that with Python sometimes it's python3 and not Python. That will work because the box will only recognize it when you write python3. So also note, like I said, sometimes it's not bin sh -i, sometimes it's bin bash -i or echo os.system bin shell that will work. So switch those around and sometimes it's, I think in more modern boxes, usr bin bash is a link to bin bash. But in some of these older boxes I think they were different. So try to switch them up. Sometimes the binaries are for the user and not just in bin bash. So look for those as well. There could be other places where that binary is that will work for you.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980&t=282)** So here's a tale of two terminals. Firefarts the guy that made the exploit, so that's why it's firefart. But you'll see ultimately I'm root, so using that upgrade ultimately led me to privilege escalation. > **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/how-to-upgrade-a-shell?u=76281980&t=378)** out of your terminal, you know, that's like very, very, very nerve-wracking. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/linux-privilege-escalation?u=76281980&t=0)** Module 9: Privilege Escalation. [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) Privilege Escalation. Our learning objectives are to understand what Linux kernel exploits are and explain the various methods to escalate privileges in Linux. So getting a shell or a foothold on a box is only half the battle. In [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md) and some of these boxes, you're going to have to escalate privileges from an unprivileged user to either root, if it's a Linux box or system, if it's a [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) box. People typically in the lab environment, I would say, rely a lot on kernel exploits and kernel exploits are great because they're -- all you have to do is enumerate the kernel version of that Linux box and hopefully, it matches up to an exploit and you can become root, which we saw with this Dirty Cow Exploit. I've done plenty of boxes where I do sudo -l and it says that you can run everything, that you have all privileges. So all you have to do is sudo su and you are the root user. Makes things really easy. I just did this on a capture the flag recently. I did sudo su and I was the root user. You can also find processes running as root and the command line lab, you found a flag running as a process. So that's to say you can also find all the processes running as root. And from there, we need to figure out if the process has some kind of if it's a SUID binary, if it's something that we can write to, and if we can write to it, then hopefully, we can run it as root and maybe put a nice reverse shell in there. So improper permissions. This is another big thing. Improper permissions in /etc/passwd. If you find that you can write to /etc/passwd, you can add a user. And as you can see here, I have this user hacker and the password here is hacker. And what I can do is if I can put this into the /etc/passwd file, I can then su hacker, password: hacker, and become the root user.

> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/linux-privilege-escalation?u=76281980&t=291)** Also, if you can view the /etc/shadow file, you can crack passwords, which we'll talk about more in the password cracking lab. But together, if I can only read the /etc/passwd file and I can only read the /etc/shadow file, I can put those together with [John the Ripper](../../Glossary/Tool/John%20the%20Ripper.md) and I can crack passwords that way. SUID binaries. So this might be hard to see, but you'll see here I'm running an enumeration script. I see SUID files, I see user /sbin/pppd. And I see that there's a vulnerability for it. I'm using sudo pppd connect and you'll see Netcat here and I now am root on the box. So SUID binaries, when you can run sudo on a binary and basically run something with root permissions, [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) that service and see if there is a privilege escalation vulnerability for that like I did here and ultimately I got root here. Cron jobs is also why I had you look at cron jobs is because if a cron job is running as root and you can edit that file, then you could, again, put a nice reverse shell in there to take out whatever's in there or add a reverse shell in there and connect to your box as root.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/linux-privilege-escalation?u=76281980&t=383)** Of course, it depends on the timing. Pspy on [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) has helped me on a lot of Hack The Box challenges and it will show you all the processes that are running. So you'll see it in kind of in nearly real time what processes are running and it colorizing it, so you can tell which ones are running as root and then see if a process is running as root and then see if you have permissions to modify that file. So we'll talk about these privilege escalation enumeration scripts at length more, but you should understand what Linux kernel exploits are, our nuclear option and then also be able to explain the various methods to escalate privileges in Linux.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/linux-privilege-escalation?u=76281980&t=477)** This is another thing that you'll get familiar with after time. As I said, getting a foothold or getting initial shell in a box is only half the battle. Privilege Escalation is a big thing as well and something that you need to get good at. So in the lab, you'll get to explore the various ways to escalate privileges to root. And that's why I put that box in there, because I think it's better that you see the various ways. I'd rather have too many vulnerabilities and you see all of them than too little. So definitely, practice on boxes in labs and get to see the various ways you can escalate privileges from becoming a unprivileged user to the root user.

#### [A word on privilege escalation enumeration scripts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/a-word-on-privilege-escalation-enumeration-scripts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/a-word-on-privilege-escalation-enumeration-scripts?u=76281980&t=1)** A [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) on Privilege Escalation Enumeration Scripts. Our learning objectives are to understand the good, the bad, and the ugly of privilege escalation scripts, and you need to decide which privilege escalation scripts that you're going to use in [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md). So why do we use privilege escalation scripts? Well, you might be asking yourself, Clint, why did you go through the [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) and [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) privilege escalation lessons so quickly about manual enumeration? Well, because we have these great scripts that we can use that does the manual enumeration work that would take us days to do in minutes or seconds. So that's to say that part of my strategy when it comes to privilege escalation is using privilege escalation scripts. I say that, but I also did a CTF recently with a buddy and I didn't use a privilege escalation script. So what I ended up doing were all these manual queries like using Dir and find string and looking in the registry. But it's good to get lost and know how to find your way back home. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/a-word-on-privilege-escalation-enumeration-scripts?u=76281980&t=95)** just like our, you know, using your [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Maps makes life a whole lot easier. > **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/a-word-on-privilege-escalation-enumeration-scripts?u=76281980&t=192)** not a big fan of it, but if it's all you have, well that's all you have. There's also the "single crack" mode. If you have a username and a password, from what I understand, "single crack" mode will try a combination of the username. Maybe it will make uppercase, lowercase or lowercase uppercase or try a one in there. So that's what "single crack" mode is. So John has a lot of functionality by default. > **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/introduction-to-password-cracking-tools?u=76281980&t=193)** It already knew that it was SHA 512. If the correct password is in rockyou.txt but it's 5,000 words down, it's going to take some time to crack that. If you have a targeted password list of 1,000 passwords, it will go through that a lot faster. So be strategic in your password lists or your wordlists. Here's some online resources. ### 11. Developing the Hacker Mindset

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding the hacker mindset](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=1)** Module 12: Developing the Hacker Mindset. Understanding the hacker mindset. Our learning objectives are to explain why the term hacker has been misunderstood, understand what it means to think like a hacker, and explain why it is important to "try smarter." So I wear the badge of hacker proudly. I think the news gets it wrong. I think dictionary definitions get it wrong. Hackers were originally people who were just curious about technology and try to figure things out. The term "cracker," though, has been the -- in essence, the bad people who try to break things and cause destruction. So hacker at the very inception of the term was never supposed to be pejorative or negative, it was supposed to be kind of a badge of honor. And you'll see here at MIT, a hacker was someone who was just interested in technology and creative. So that was a positive thing from the inception of the term "hacker." So I'm part of a group called Hacking is not a Crime, and we basically try to spread the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) that a hacker is not a bad person. When the news says a hacker broke into this and they show people with the hood on and gloves because, you know, you got to wear gloves when you type, that's definitely the wrong image. People -- hackers, we are all different types of people. We come from all different walks of life and we're trying to spread the word that being a hacker is a good thing. And there's a whole bunch of us who do really great things for companies.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=97)** So the hacker mindset is learning to think. I say think laterally. It's if you keep running into the same problem, you know, you have to think of how to get around it. If there's a giant wall there, you're not running and hitting your head against the wall every single time, maybe there's another way around the wall. Maybe not just laterally, but underneath or over, you know. So this is how a hacker thinks. They don't think step by step by step by step. There's no manual. I took two classes in grad school. One was network defenses, which was like a lab manual of how to set up a firewall or how to use Splunk. And then I took a hacking class and it was like, here's the lab environment, figure it out. And I fell in love with the hacker mindset. I fell in love with the, you know, this is your time to think how to solve this problem, this puzzle. And I really, really, really loved it and became addicted. And that's why it's my career now. So from being a cop and being the military, you know, you learn from instructions. You learn from the law. These are the things that you have to do. I will say, though, that I think cops have some of the best hacker mindset because no problem that you encounter as a police officer is the same. Everything is going to be different. And you need to think quickly about a problem because people's lives are on the line, essentially, when you come and address a situation. So in essence, you know, I know a lot of cops become forensic people. I think cops have a great mindset for hacking because you have to think around the problem.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=191)** And I think cops do that. You know, military can be the same way depending on what your job is in the military. But yes, there are definitely jobs out there that are very line by line by line in the instruction manual have to follow X, Y, and Z. So that's to say when we go to school and we learn all these things and structure, you know, it kind of gives you a disservice when it comes to thinking strategically and laterally around problems and becoming mentally [agile](../../Skills/DevOps/Agile%20Development.md). So if you've been a hacker and you've been in these CTFs and even if you've done this professionally, you fail, you fail a lot. And the people that make it are the people who fail and keep going. If you're someone that fail -- doesn't like failure, you're not going to be a very good hacker. I don't think you'll make it in the industry. You know, I'm not trying to be mean here. I'm just saying that if you don't like to fail, you know, maybe find another job. Because as hackers, we fail a lot. And, you know, I think people who are Pentesters and hackers can relate to this because, you know, you try your cross-site scripting payload for the hundredth time and one tweak is all it takes to make that fire. So, you know, that's to say that keep going. You know, failure should drive you forward. So OffSec says "try harder," try smarter. And this is what I talk about where enumeration is so important. If you're on a web server and you're putting an ASP shell on something that has [PHP](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PHP.md), is not going to work,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=285)** or if I'm trying to do remote code execution on a [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) box and I'm catting /etc/passwd and it's not working, well, why am I doing that? I'm not being smart about my enumeration. If I knew it was a Windows box, then I would know that catting /etc/passwd is not going to work. So think about that. You're going to be nervous and you're going to be tired in [OSCP](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/OSCP.md). So I don't blame you if you do that by accident. But if -- perfect practice makes perfect. So if we're practicing and we're realizing, hey, off the bat, this thing has PHP in it, I know I need to use a PHP shell and not ASP. Then when it comes time that you're stressed out, you're going to operate at the level of your training. Some people are great hackers. Some people are hackers at a very young age and they learn and they're very curious and they're very successful and other people need an instruction manual. I, you know, nothing against people who have been in the IT industry for 20 years. I hear people say, I've been in it for 20 years and I have to give them literally step by step instructions on how to do something because they can't think outside of that. They need to look at the manual and just don't understand it. If, you know, they can't skip Step 2, if I say here's one, two, and three and something is wrong in two, I can't do this instruction manual because I don't -- I can't figure out Number 2. You know, you have to think outside the box. So in summary, I've explained why the term "hacker" has been
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/understanding-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=380)** misunderstood. We should understand what it means to think like a hacker and explain why it is important to "try smarter."

Because I remember when I got to the company I'm working in now and I've been doing things like OSCP and CTFs, and the objective was always to get root on the box, whereas in the bug bounty world, it's, hey, I found this vulnerability in your web app, you know, pay me for it. >
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/offensive-penetration-testing/tips-on-harnessing-the-hacker-mindset?u=76281980&t=375)** when all I needed to do was go back and check bad characters. If you've taken this course, you know that I told you to check for bad characters, right, over and over again. So something that could have taken me an hour, took me six times as long because I didn't go back to the basics and check things that were very simple and I should have seen. So in summary, we should now understand the best practices when it comes to harnessing the hacker mindset.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
